010                                               A BSTRACT
011            Adam has become one of the most popular optimizers for training modern deep
012            neural networks, such as transformers. However, its applicability is largely re-
013            stricted to single-level optimization problems. In this paper, we aim to extend
014            vanilla Adam to tackle bilevel optimization problems, which have important ap-
015            plications in machine learning, such as meta-learning. In particular, we study
016            stochastic bilevel optimization problems where the lower-level function is strongly
017
               convex and the upper-level objective is nonconvex with potentially unbounded
               smoothness. This unbounded smooth objective function covers a broad class of
018
               neural networks, including transformers, which may exhibit non-Lipschitz gradi-
019
               ents. In this work, we first introduce AdamBO, a single-loop Adam-type method
020                           e −4 ) oracle complexity to find ϵ-stationary points, where the or-
               that achieves O(ϵ
021
               acle calls involve stochastic gradient or Hessian/Jacobian-vector product evalu-
022            ations. The key to our analysis is a novel randomness decoupling lemma that
023            provides refined control over the lower-level variable. Additionally, we propose
024            VR-AdamBO, a variance-reduced version with an improved oracle complexity of
025             e −3 ). The improved analysis is based on a novel stopping time approach and
               O(ϵ
026            a careful treatment of the lower-level error. We conduct extensive experiments
027            on various machine learning tasks involving bilevel formulations with recurrent
028            neural networks (RNNs) and transformers, demonstrating the effectiveness of our
029            proposed Adam-type algorithms.
030
031   1   I NTRODUCTION
032
033   The Adam algorithm (Kingma & Ba, 2014) is one of the most popular optimizers for training mod-
034
      ern deep neural networks due to their computational efficiency and minimal need for hyperparameter
      tuning. For example, Adam has become the default choice for training transformers (Vaswani et al.,
035
      2017; Devlin et al., 2018) and vision transformers (ViT) (Dosovitskiy et al., 2021). Practitioners fa-
036
      vor Adam and adaptive gradient methods in general because they significantly outperform stochas-
037   tic gradient descent (SGD) for certain models, such as transformers (Zhang et al., 2019; Crawshaw
038   et al., 2022; Kunstner et al., 2023; Ahn et al., 2023). Recently, there is a line of work analyzing the
039   convergence of Adam under various assumptions (Guo et al., 2021b; Défossez et al., 2020; Wang
040   et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2022; Li et al., 2023a).
041
      Despite the empirical and theoretical advances of Adam, it is only applicable for single-level op-
042
      timization problems such as the empirical risk minimization. However, there is a huge class of
043   machine learning problems which are inherently bilevel optimization problems (Bracken & McGill,
044   1973; Dempe, 2002), including meta-learning (Franceschi et al., 2018; Rajeswaran et al., 2019),
045   reinforcement learning (Konda & Tsitsiklis, 2000), hyperparameter optimization (Franceschi et al.,
046   2018; Feurer & Hutter, 2019) and continual learning (Borsos et al., 2020; Hao et al., 2023). There-
047   fore, an important question arises: How can we extend the applicability of vanilla Adam to solve
048   bilevel optimization problems, while ensuring both provable theoretical convergence guaran-
049   tees and strong empirical performance for machine learning applications?
050   In this paper, we provide a positive answer to this question, under the setting of bilevel optimiza-
051   tion under unbounded smoothness (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a). In particular, the bilevel
052   optimization in this setting has the following form:
053                        min Φ(x) := f (x, y ∗ (x)), s.t., y ∗ (x) ∈ arg min g(x, y),                (1)
                         x∈Rdx                                           y∈Rdy


                                                        1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      where f and g are upper- and lower-level functions respectively, and f satisfies a unbounded smooth-
055   ness condition (see Definition 3.1) and g is a strongly-convex function in y. One example satisfying
056   this particular setting is meta-learning (Finn et al., 2017; Franceschi et al., 2018) with certain ma-
057   chine learning models such as RNNs (Elman, 1990) or transformers (Vaswani et al., 2017), where
058   x represents all layers except for the prediction head, y represents the prediction head, and the goal
059   is to learn the shared model parameter x to find a common representation such that it can quickly
060   adapt to various tasks by simply adjusting the task-specific prediction head y. The unbounded
061   smoothness condition for the upper-level function f is particularly relevant in this paper for two
062   main reasons. First, recent studies have demonstrated that the gradient’s Lipschitz constant (i.e., the
063
      smoothness constant) is unbounded in various modern neural networks, including RNNs and trans-
      formers (Zhang et al., 2020b; Crawshaw et al., 2022; Hao et al., 2024). Second, Adam is empiri-
064
      cally successful on training these neural networks (Vaswani et al., 2017; Kunstner et al., 2023) and
065
      its convergence under unbounded smoothness was recently proved within the single-level optimiza-
066   tion framework (Li et al., 2023a). Therefore it is natural and imperative to design new Adam-type
067   algorithms, building on the vanilla Adam approach, to solve bilevel optimization problems in the
068   unbounded smoothness setting.
069
      We introduce two Adam-type algorithms for such bilevel optimization problems with provable con-
070
      vergence guarantees. The first algorithm is called Adam for Bilevel Optimization (AdamBO).
071
      AdamBO begins by running a few iterations of SGD to warm-start the lower-level variable, after
072   which it simultaneously applies vanilla Adam updates to the upper-level variable and SGD updates
073   to the lower-level variable. The primary challenge for the convergence analysis of AdamBO is tack-
074   ling the complicated dependency between the upper-level hypergradient bias and the lower-level
075   estimation error when the upper-level performs the vanilla Adam update. The convergence analysis
076   of AdamBO for unbounded smooth upper-level functions builds upon the insight of regarding bilevel
077   optimization as a stochastic optimization problem under distributional drift (Gong et al., 2024a), but
078   with a few important differences. First, our analysis incorporates a novel randomness decoupling
079   lemma for lower-level error control, which arises from using Adam updates for the upper-level vari-
080
      able. Second, unlike (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a), the lower-level error in our setting is
      not necessarily small across iterations, requiring a more refined analysis to handle the hypergradi-
081
      ent bias and establish convergence guarantees. In addition, we also introduce another Adam-type
082
      algorithm, namely VR-AdamBO, by incorporating the variance reduction techniques (Cutkosky &
083   Orabona, 2019) along with Adam for the upper-level variable and the lower-level acceleration tech-
084   niques (Gong et al., 2024b) to further improve the convergence rate. The analysis for VR-AdamBO
085   relies on a novel stopping-time analysis in the context of bilevel optimization and a careful treatment
086   for the lower-level error, which is different from the techniques used in single-level variance-reduced
087   Adam (Li et al., 2023a). Our main contributions are summarized as follows.
088
             • We design a variant of Adam, called AdamBO, for solving bilevel optimization problems
089            under the unbounded smoothness setting. We prove that AdamBO converges to ϵ-stationary
090                         e −4 ) oracle complexity. To achieve this result, we develop a novel ran-
               points with O(ϵ
091            domness decoupling lemma for lower-level error control and a refined analysis for the
092            hypergradient bias, which are of independent interest and could be applied to analyzing the
093            convergence of other adaptive optimizers in bilevel optimization.
094
             • We propose a variance-reduced variant of AdamBO, named VR-AdamBO, with an im-
095                                          e −3 ). The proof relies on a novel stopping time analysis in
               proved oracle complexity of O(ϵ
096
               the context of bilevel optimization and a careful treatment for the lower-level error.
097
             • We conduct experiments on deep AUC maximization and meta-learning for text classifica-
098
               tion tasks with RNNs and transformers to verify the effectiveness of the proposed Adam-
099
               type algorithms. We show that both AdamBO and VR-AdamBO consistently outperform
100            other bilevel algorithms during the training process. Notably, for the transformer model,
101            they improve the training (testing) AUC by at least 14% (7%) over other baselines. The
102            running time results indicate that our algorithms converge much faster than baselines.
103
104   2   R ELATED W ORK
105
106   Convergence Analysis of Adam. Adam was proposed by (Kingma & Ba, 2014) and the conver-
107   gence guarantee was established under the framework of online convex optimization. Reddi et al.
      (2019) identified a divergence example of Adam under fixed hyperparameters and designed new


                                                        2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      variants to fix the divergence issue of Adam. Recently, there is a line of work analyzing the conver-
109   gence of Adam under various assumptions and problem-dependent hyperparameter choices (Zhou
110   et al., 2018; Guo et al., 2021b; Défossez et al., 2020; Wang et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2022; Li et al.,
111   2023a). The most related work to our paper is (Li et al., 2023a), which studied the convergence
112   of Adam under relaxed assumptions (i.e., generalized smoothness as defined by (Li et al., 2023a)).
113   However, all of these works only consider Adam within the single-level optimization framework and
114   are not applicable for bilevel optimization problems.
115   Bilevel Optimization. Bilevel optimization was extensively studied in the literature, most of which
116   focus on asymptotic convergence guarantees (Bracken & McGill, 1973; Vicente et al., 1994; Anan-
117   dalingam & White, 1990; White & Anandalingam, 1993). Ghadimi & Wang (2018) studied bilevel
118   optimization algorithms with non-asymptotic convergence guarantees when the lower-level func-
119   tion is strongly convex. The complexity results were later improved by a series of work (Hong et al.,
120   2023; Ji et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2021; Dagréou et al., 2022; Kwon et al., 2023; Chen et al., 2023a).
121   When each realization of the functions has a Lipschitz stochastic gradient, several works incorporate
122   momentum-based variance reduction techniques (Cutkosky & Orabona, 2019) to further improve the
123
      convergence rate (Khanduri et al., 2021; Guo et al., 2021a; Yang et al., 2021). Recently, (Hao et al.,
      2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b) considered bilevel optimization with unbounded smoothness for the
124
      upper-level function and designed stochastic algorithms with convergence guarantees. However,
125
      none of these works use the Adam update under the bilevel optimization setting.
126
127   Relaxed Smoothness. Zhang et al. (2020b) initiated the convergence analysis of the gradient clip-
128   ping algorithms under the relaxed smoothness condition, which was motivated by the loss landscape
129
      of RNNs and LSTMs. The work of (Zhang et al., 2020b) inspired a line of work focusing on design-
      ing various algorithms under the relaxed smoothness condition (Zhang et al., 2020a; Jin et al., 2021;
130
      Liu et al., 2022; Crawshaw et al., 2023a;b; Faw et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2023; Li et al., 2023a;b),
131
      some of them achieved improved convergence rates (Liu et al., 2023; Reisizadeh et al., 2023; Li
132   et al., 2023a). Several variants of relaxed smoothness were considered in (Crawshaw et al., 2022;
133   Chen et al., 2023b; Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b). This work considered the same problem
134   setting as in (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b), focusing on designing Adam-type algorithms
135   for bilevel optimization with unbounded smooth upper-level functions.
136

143   where f (x, y) = Eξ∼Df [F (x, y; ξ)] and g(x, y) = Eζ∼Dg [G(x, y; ζ)], with Df and Dg being the
144   underlying data distributions, respectively. When the lower-level function is strongly convex, the
145   hypergradient has the following form (Ghadimi & Wang, 2018):

      as a generalization of the relaxed smooth assumption in (Zhang et al., 2020b) and the coordinate-
163   wise relaxed smoothness assumption in (Crawshaw et al., 2022). Moreover, it has been empirically
164   verified for bilevel formulations with RNNs (Hao et al., 2024).

      Assumption 3.2. Suppose the followings hold for functions f and g: (i) f is continuously differ-
166   entiable and (Lx,0 , Lx,1 , Ly,0 , Ly,1 )-smooth in (x, y); (ii) For every x, ∥∇y f (x, y ∗ (x))∥ ≤ lf,0 ;
167   (iii) For every x, g(x, y) is µ-strongly convex in y for µ > 0; (iv) g is continuously differentiable
168   and lg,1 -smooth jointly in (x, y); (v) g is twice continuously differentiable, and ∇2xy g, ∇2yy g are
169   lg,2 -Lipschitz jointly in (x, y); (vi) Objective function Φ is bounded from below by Φ∗ .
170
171   Remark: Assumption 3.2 is standard in the bilevel optimization literature (Kwon et al., 2023;
172
      Ghadimi & Wang, 2018; Hao et al., 2024). Under this assumption, the objective function Φ is
      (L0 , L1 )-smooth, see Lemma B.10 in Appendix B for definitions of L0 , L1 and more details.
173

175

178   Remark: Assumption 3.3 assumes the noise in the stochastic gradient and Hessian/Jacobian is
179   almost-surely bounded or light-tailed. This is an standard assumption in the literature of optimiza-
180   tion for single-level relaxed smooth functions (Zhang et al., 2020b;a), as well as for bilevel opti-
181   mization under unbounded smooth upper-level functions (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b).

      Assumption 3.4. (i) Let z = (x, y) and z ′ = (x′ , y ′ ), if ∥z − z ′ ∥ ≤ 1/ L2x,1 + L2y,1 , then for every
183

185   satisfy Assumption 3.2 (iv) and (v).
186   Remark: Assumption 3.4 (i) requires that certain properties of the second argument (i.e., the lower-
187   level variable y) in the upper-level function at the population level also hold almost surely for each
188   random realization. Assumption 3.4 (ii) requires each random realization of the lower-level function
189   satisfies the same property as in the population level. Similar assumptions were made implicitly
190   in the bilevel optimization literature (Ghadimi & Wang, 2018). Note that this assumption does not
191   assume any properties in terms of the upper-level variable x under each random realization.
192   Assumption 3.5. F (x, y; ξ) and G(x, y; ζ) satisfy Assumption 3.2 for every ξ and ζ almost surely.
193
194
      Remark: Assumption 3.5 poses a strictly stronger requirement than Assumption 3.4: it assumes
      each random realization for both upper-level and lower-level function has the same property as in
195
      the population level. This assumption has been shown to be necessary to obtain the improved oracle
196
      complexity O(ϵ−3 ) for both single-level problems (Arjevani et al., 2023; Cutkosky & Orabona,
197   2019) and bilevel problems (Khanduri et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2021; Gong et al., 2024b).

199   4     A DAM BO AND C ONVERGENCE A NALYSIS
200
201   4.1   A LGORITHM D ESIGN , M AIN C HALLENGES , AND T ECHNIQUE OVERVIEW
202
      Algorithm Design. Our first Adam-type algorithm AdamBO is presented in Algorithm 1. It consists
203
      of the following components. First, the algorithm requires several warm-start steps for updating
204   the lower-level variable y for a given initialization of the upper-level variable x0 (line 2), which
205   is designed to obtain a good estimate of the optimal lower-level variable at the very beginning
206   and shares the same spirit of the bilevel algorithms introduced in (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al.,
207   2024a;b). Second, the algorithm updates both the upper- and lower-level variables simultaneously:
208   the lower-level variable y is updated by SGD, and the upper-level variable x is updated by the vanilla
209   Adam algorithm (lines 3 ∼ 9). Therefore, the upper-level update benefits from the coordinate-wise
210   adaptive learning rate. In contrast, the existing bilevel optimization algorithms under the unbounded
211   smoothness setting use normalized SGD with momentum to update the upper-level variable (Hao
212
      et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b), which use a universal learning rate for every coordinate.
213   Main Challenges. The main challenges for the convergence analysis of AdamBO are listed as
214   follows. First, the analysis of vanilla Adam in the single-level generalized smooth optimization set-
215   ting (Li et al., 2023a) is not directly applicable for bilevel problems. This is because the hypergra-
      dient estimator in bilevel optimization may have a non-negligible bias due to inaccurate estimation

230   of the lower-level variable, whereas the single-level analysis in (Li et al., 2023a) does not need to
231   account for this issue. Second, the existing algorithms and analyses for bilevel optimization with
232   unbounded smooth upper-level functions require the lower-level error to be small (Hao et al., 2024;
233   Gong et al., 2024a;b), which may not hold for AdamBO. In particular, the existing analysis crucially
234
      relies on a fixed update length for the upper-level variable at every iteration (due to normalization):
      the analysis in (Hao et al., 2024; Gong et al., 2024a;b) views the update of the upper-level variable
235
      as a fixed distributional drift for the lower-level function, which is crucial to show that the lower-
236
      level error is small and the hypergradient bias is negligible. However, such an argument is not true
237   for AdamBO: the Adam update for the lower-level variable does not have a fixed update size and
238   it depends on randomness from both upper-level and lower-level random variables in the stochastic
239   setting, which make the lower-level error control more challenging.
240
      Technique Overview. To address these challenges, one of our main technical contributions is the
241
      introduction of a novel randomness decoupling lemma for controlling the lower-level error when
242
      the upper-level variable is updated by Adam, as illustrated in Section 4.3.2. This lemma provide a
243   high probability guarantee for the lower-level error control when the upper-level update rule satisfies
244   certain conditions (which are satisfied by the vanilla Adam update rule for the upper-level variable).
245   The key novelty of this lemma lies in the randomness-decoupling fact: the high-probability bound
246   depends solely on the randomness {ζt }Tt=1 from the lower-level random variables, and it holds for
247   any fixed sequence of upper-level variables {xt }Tt=1 and any fixed upper-level random variables
248   {ξ¯t }Tt=1 that respect the Adam updates. To describe the condition that Adam satisfies and to prove
249   this lemma, we introduce an auxiliary sequence (defined in (3)) that separates the randomness in
250   the upper- and lower-level random variables, which is new and has not been leveraged in previous
251
      bilevel optimization literature.

277   4.3     P ROOF S KETCH
278
279   In this section, we provide a proof sketch for Theorem 4.1. The detailed proof can be found in
280   Appendix D. Let yt∗ = y ∗ (xt ). The key idea is to provide a high probability bound of lower-
281
      level estimation error ∥yt − yt∗ ∥ when the upper-level variable x is updated by the vanilla Adam.
      Lemma 4.4 provides such a guarantee: the lower-level error ∥yt −yt∗ ∥ is bounded by a function of the
282
      initial estimation error ∥y1 − y1∗ ∥, the variance term σg,1
                                                                 2

      4.3.2    R ANDOM D ECOUPLING L EMMA FOR L OWER -L EVEL E RROR C ONTROL
296   In this section, we introduce the random decoupling lemma (Lemma 4.2) for the lower-level error
297   control. The rationale is as follows: for any given upper-level variable sequence and any given
298   randomness from the upper-level updates that satisfy certain conditions and are consistent with the
299   AdamBO updates, we can bound the lower-level error with high probability, where the randomness
300   is taken solely from lower-level random variables. Specifically, for any given sequence {x̃t }, define
301   ζ̃t and ξˆt as the random variables from the lower-level and upper-level, respectively, at the t-th
302   iteration (see (26) for definition). We consider the following update rule for {ỹt }, which is exactly
303   SGD and corresponds to line 5 of Algorithm 1:
304                                           ỹt+1 = ỹt − γ∇y G(x̃t , ỹt ; ζ̃t ).                                     (2)
305
306   Let ỹt∗ = y ∗ (x̃t ) and F̃ty = σ(ζ̃1 , . . . , ζ̃t−1 ). Denote G̃t := maxk≤t ∥∇Φ(x̃k )∥, L̃t := L0 + L1 G̃t .
307   We also introduce the following auxiliary sequences {m̃t } and {ũt } for our analysis:
308            m̃t = (1 − αt )m̃t−1 + αt ∇ϕ(x̃  ˆ                   ˆ                               ˆ

      Remark: Lemma 4.2 shows that, when (4) holds for any sequence {x̃t } and any {ξˆt } (as satisfied
325   by the vanilla Adam update for the upper-level variable), the lower-level error can be controlled with
326   high probability as in (5). In addition, the high probability is taken over the randomness solely from
327   the lower-level filtration F̃Ty +1 . This lemma provides a technical tool to control the lower-level error
328   without concerns about the dependency issues from the upper-level randomness. In particular, the
329   right-hand side of (5) consists of two parts: the standard variance term, which does not involve the
330   update of {x̃t } over t; and the drift terms, which account for the update of {x̃t } over time.

      5.1        A LGORITHM D ESIGN
372
373   In this section, we propose a variance-reduced version of AdamBO, called VR-AdamBO, as shown
374   in Algorithm 2. Similar to AdamBO, the VR-AdamBO algorithm also includes a warm-start phase
375   for the lower-level variable (line 2): it runs stochastic Nesterov accelerated gradient (SNAG) method
376   on the lower-level variable y for T0 iterations, with the initial upper-level variable x0 fixed. After
377   that, VR-AdamBO updates the upper-level variable by VRAdam (Li et al., 2023a) (i.e., the Adam
      algorithm (Kingma & Ba, 2014) with recursive momentum (Cutkosky & Orabona, 2019), lines

407   algorithm in (Gong et al., 2024b), with the key difference being that AccBO uses normalized SGD

      with momentum for the upper-level update, whereas VR-AdamBO employs VRAdam.

425
      5.3    P ROOF S KETCH
426
427   In this section, we briefly discuss the challenges in analyzing VR-AdamBO and provide a roadmap
428   for the proof. The detailed proofs can be found in Appendix E. Note that to apply relaxed smoothness
429   property and descent inequality, as listed in Lemmas B.10 and B.11, one requirement is that xt+1
430   and xt should remain close since Definition 3.1 is a local condition rather than a global one. For
431   AdamBO, this requirement is not hard to satisfy with sufficiently small η, based on Lemma 4.5 and
      its remark below. However, VR-AdamBO may not satisfy such a almost sure bound for ∥mt ∥ due


                                                              8
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


432
      to the S TORM-type update (Cutkosky & Orabona, 2019) for the upper-level variable. To overcome
433   this difficulty, we introduce a novel stopping time approach in the context of bilevel optimization.
434                                           ˆ              ¯
      Specifically, we define ϵt := mt − Et [∇ϕ(x  t , ŷt ; ξt )] and the new stopping time τ as
435
436                       τ := min{t | ∥∇Φ(xt )∥ ≤ G} ∧ min{t | ∥ϵt ∥ ≥ G} ∧ (T + 1),                        (7)
437   where G is specified in Theorem 5.1. It is worth noting that our definition of τ for the analysis of
438   VR-AdamBO differs from that in (Li et al., 2023a) for VRAdam, as our ϵt is not defined as the
439   difference between mt and ∇Φ(xt ). At this point, we may still fail to guarantee the boundedness of
440   ∥mt ∥ before time τ , unless the hypergradient bias introduced by the lower-level estimation error can
441   be effectively controlled. Fortunately, by leveraging the lower-level acceleration technique (Gong
442   et al., 2024b) with periodic updates and averaging, we develop a new induction argument (i.e., Lem-
443
      mas E.10 to E.12) to show that under t < τ and some good event Ey , both ∥ŷt − yt∗ ∥ and ∥mt ∥
      are bounded. We then show the averaged lower-level error is small under the parameter choices in
444
      Theorem 5.1 (see Lemma E.4), which shares an similar spirit as Lemma 4.6. Combining aforemen-
445
      tioned lemmas with the technique developed in (Li et al., 2023a) for the upper-level analysis, we
446   obtain the improved complexity result. One can refer to Appendix E for more details.
447
448   6   E XPERIMENTS
449
450   Deep AUC Maximization with RNNs/Transformers. The Area Under the ROC Curve
451   (AUC) (Hanley & McNeil, 1983) is a widely used metric for evaluating the effectiveness of binary
452
      classification models, especially in the imbalanced data scenarios. It is defined as the probability
      that the prediction score of a positive example is higher than that of a negative example (Hanley &
453
      McNeil, 1982). Deep AUC maximization (Liu et al., 2020; Ying et al., 2016) can be formulated as
454
      a min-max optimization problem (Liu et al., 2020): minw∈Rd ,(a,b)∈R2 maxα∈R f (w, a, b, α) :=
455
      Ez [F (w, a, b, α; z)], where F (w, a, b, α; z) = (1 − p)(h(w; x) − a)2 I[c=1] + p(h(w; x) −
456
      b)2 I[c=−1] + 2(1 + α)(ph(w; x)I[c=−1] − (1 − p)h(w; x)I[c=1] ) − p(1 − p)α2 , w denotes the
457
      model parameter of a deep neural network, and z = (x, c) represents a random training data sample
458
      (x represents the feature vector and c ∈ {+1, −1} represents the class label), the function h(w, x)
459
      is a scoring function for the sample with feature x, and p = Pr(c = 1) indicates the proportion
460   of positive samples in the population. This min-max problem can be reformulated as the form of a
461   bilevel optimization problem with lower-level objective function g = −f :
462
          min          Ez [F (w, a, b, α∗ (w, a, b); z)]   s.t., α∗ (w, a, b) ∈ arg min −Ez [F (w, a, b, α; z)].
463   w∈Rd ,(a,b)∈R2                                                               α∈R
464
465
      In above, (w, a, b) is the upper-level variable, and α is the lower-level variable. The lower-level
      problem is a strongly convex one-dimensional quadratic function with respect to α, while the upper-
466
      level objective is non-convex and can exhibit unbounded smoothness when using a recurrent neural
467
      network or a transformer as the predictive model (Crawshaw et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2020b).
468
469   In our experiment, we focus on tackling an imbalanced text classification task by maximizing the
470   AUC metric. Specifically, we conduct experiments using deep AUC maximization on the imbal-
471
      anced Sentiment140 dataset (Go et al., 2009), a binary text classification benchmark. Following
      the approach in (Yuan et al., 2021), we introduce imbalance in the training set using a pre-specified
472
      imbalance ratio (p) while keeping the test set distribution unchanged. For a given p, we randomly
473
      remove positive samples (labeled as 1) from the training set until the desired proportion of positive
474   examples is achieved. In our experiment, we set p to 0.8 (0.9), meaning that 80% (90%) of the train-
475   ing samples are positive examples. We run the experiment using two different models, a two-layer
476   transformer, and a two-layer recurrent neural network (RNN) with the same input dimension of 300,
477   hidden dimension of 4096, and an output dimension of 2.
478
      To evaluate the effectiveness of our proposed bilevel optimization algorithm, we compare with recent
479
      bilevel optimization baselines, including StocBio (Ji et al., 2021), TTSA (Hong et al., 2023), SABA
480   (Dagréou et al., 2022), MA-SOBA (Chen et al., 2023a), SUSTAIN (Khanduri et al., 2021), VRBO
481   (Yang et al., 2021), BO-REP (Hao et al., 2024), SLIP (Gong et al., 2024a), and AccBO (Gong et al.,
482   2024b). The training and testing results of the transformer model over 50 epochs are presented in
483   Figure 3 (a) and (b), while the corresponding running times are shown in Figure 3 (c) and (d). Our
484   proposed Adam-type algorithms, AdamBO and VR-AdamBO, show the faster convergence rate
485   and significantly outperform other baselines. In particular, the performance on the training AUC
      (testing AUC) is better by at least 14% (7%) over other baselines. The running time results indicate


                                                             9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

506   that AdamBO and VR-AdamBO converge much faster to a high AUC value compared to the other
507   baselines. We also perform the AUC maximization on a RNN model with imbalance rario of 0.8,
508   and the results are presented in Appendix F.1. More detailed parameter tuning and selection can be
509   found in Appendix F.
510   Hyper-representation Learning. Hyper-representation learning, i.e., meta-learning (Finn et al.,
511   2017), aims to find a good meta learner parameterized by x, such that it can quickly adapt to a
512   new task i by fine-tuning the corresponding adapter yi . Consider a meta-learning task consisting
513   of K tasks with the training set {Ditr | i = 1, . . . , K} and validation set {Dival | i = 1, . . . , K}.
514   Each task has a loss function L(x, yi ; ξi ) over each sample ξi . This meta-learning problem can
515   be reformulated as a bilevel optimization, where the lower-level objective function tries to find an
516   optimal task-specific adapter yi∗ (x) on training data Ditr , and the upper-level minimizes the objective
517   function on validation data Dival by finding the optimal meta-learner x with a set of adapters y =
518   {y1∗ (x), y2∗ (x), . . . , yK
                                  ∗
                                    (x)}. We have the following formulation:
519                                 K                                                             K
                                 1 X 1          X
                                                            ∗                  ∗               1 X                      µ
520        min                                       L(x, y   (x); ξ), s.t., y   (x) = arg min       LDitr (x, yi ; ζ) + ∥yi ∥2 ,
521                x             K i=1 |Dival |  val
                                                                                           y   K i=1
                                                                                                                        2
                                                                 ξ∈Di
522
      where LDitr (x, yi ; ζ) = |D1tr | ζ∈Dtr L(x, yi ; ζ). The adapter (parameterized by yi ) is typically
                                        P
523
                                    i          i
524   instantiated as the last linear layer, and the meta learner (parameterized by x) is the remaining layers
525   of model, which guarantees that the lower-level function to be strongly-convex when µ > 0.
526
      We conduct the meta-learning experiments for the text classification on dataset Stanford Natural
527
      Language Inference (SNLI) (Bowman et al., 2015), which consists of 570k pairs of sentences with 3
528   classes. We construct K = 500 tasks, where each task Ditr and Dival randomly sample two disjoint
529   categories from the original data, respectively. Empirically, we use mini-batches of meta-tasks for
530   training, with a task batch size of 25. A 3-layer recurrent network is used as representation layers and
531   a fully-connected layer as an adapter. The input dimension, hidden dimension and output dimension
532   are set to be 300, 4096, and 3, respectively.
533
      We compare with typical meta-learning algorithms, MAML (Rajeswaran et al., 2019) and ANIL
534   (Raghu et al., 2019), and recent bilevel optimization algorithms, StocBio (Ji et al., 2021), TTSA
535   (Hong et al., 2023), SABA (Dagréou et al., 2022), MA-SOBA (Chen et al., 2023a), BO-REP (Hao
536   et al., 2024), SLIP (Gong et al., 2024a). The comparison results of training and testing accuracy are
537   shown in Figure 2. AdamBO outperforms other baselines on training set, and exhibits faster con-
538   vergence rate. One can refer to Appendix F for detailed hyper-parameter choices and experimental
539   settings. All the experiments are run on an single NVIDIA A6000 (48GB memory) GPU and a
      AMD EPYC 7513 32-Core CPU.

894   A     E QUIVALENT U PDATE RULE OF A DAM BO (A LGORITHM 1)
895
896   In this section, we aim to provide a simplified version of the bias correction steps (lines 7-8) of
897   Algorithm 1. Inspired by (Li et al., 2023a, Appendix C.1), we present an equivalent yet simpler
898   update rule of Algorithm 1 in the following Proposition A.1. The detailed equivalent framework is
899   also outlined in Algorithm 4.

1111
       In this section, we provide a well-known probabilistic lemma without proof.
1112   Lemma B.8 (Optional Stopping Theorem). Let {Zt }t≥1 be a martingale with respect to a filtration
1113   {Ft }t≥0 . Let τ be a bounded stopping time with respect to the same filtration. Then we have
1114   E[Zτ ] = E[Z0 ].

1445   C      P ROOF OF THE R ANDOM D ECOUPLING L EMMA (L EMMA 4.2)
1446
1447   C.1     R ECURSIVE C ONTROL ON M OMENT G ENERATING F UNCTION
1448
1449   The following technical lemma on recursive control is crucial for establishing high probability guar-
1450   antee for controlling the lower-level estimation error at anytime. We follow a similar argument as
1451   in (Cutler et al., 2023, Proposition 29) with a slight generalization.
1452   Proposition C.1 (Recursive control on MGF). Consider scalar stochastic processes (Vt ), (Dt ),
1453   (Xt ) and (Yt ) on a probability space with filtration (Ht ), which are linked by the inequality
1454                                                      p
1455
                                      Vt+1 ≤ ρt Vt + Dt Vt + Xt + Yt + κt                               (22)
1456   for some deterministic constants ρt ∈ (−∞, 1] and κt ∈ R. Suppose the following properties hold.
1457
               • Vt and Yt are non-negative and Ht -measurable.


                                                              27
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1458
              • Dt is mean-zero sub-Gaussian conditioned on Ht with deterministic parameter σt :
1459
1460                                E[exp(θDt ) | Ht ] ≤ exp(θ2 σt2 /2) for all θ ∈ R.
1461
1462          • Xt is non-negative and sub-exponential conditioned on Ht with deterministic parameter
1463            νt :
1464                            E[exp(θXt ) | Ht ] ≤ exp(θνt ) for all 0 ≤ θ ≤ 1/νt .
1465
1466
       Then the estimate
1467                    E[exp(θVt+1 )] ≤ exp(θ(νt + κt ))E[exp(θ((1 + ρt )Vt /2 + Yt ))]
1468                                         n            o
1469   holds for any θ satisfying 0 ≤ θ ≤ min 1−ρ
                                               2σ 2
                                                   t
                                                     ,  1
                                                       2νt .
                                                         t
1470
1471   Proof of Proposition C.1. For any index t ≥ 0 and any scalar θ ≥ 0, the law of total expectation
1472   implies
1473                             h                  p                     i
1474         E[exp(θVt+1 )] ≤ E exp θ ρt Vt + Dt Vt + Xt + Yt + κt
1475                                      h                   h        p                     ii
1476                         = exp(θκt )E exp(θ(ρt Vt + Yt ))E exp(θDt Vt ) exp(θXt ) | Ht .
1477
1478   Hölder’s inequality in turn yields
1479          h       p                   i r h             p          i
1480         E exp(θDt Vt ) exp(θXt ) | Ht ≤ E exp(2θDt Vt ) | Ht · E [exp(2θXt ) | Ht ]
1481                                        q
1482                                       ≤ exp(2θ2 σt2 Vt ) exp(2θνt )
1483
                                                     = exp(θ2 σt2 Vt ) exp(θνt )
1484
1485   provided 0 ≤ θ ≤ 2ν1 t . Therefore, if θ satisfies
1486
1487                                                             1 − ρt 1
                                             0 ≤ θ ≤ min               ,           ,
1488                                                              2σt2 2νt
1489
       then the following estimate holds for all t ≥ 0:
1490
                     E[exp(θVt+1 )] ≤ exp(θκt )E exp(θ(ρt Vt + Yt )) exp(θ2 σt2 Vt ) exp(θνt )

1491
1492
                                    = exp(θ(νt + κt ))E exp(θ((ρt + θσt2 )Vt + Yt ))

1493
1494                                 ≤ exp(θ(νt + κt ))E [exp(θ((1 + ρt )Vt /2 + Yt ))] ,
1495   where the last inequality uses the given range of θ. Thus the proof is completed.
1496
1497
       C.2   P ROOF OF L EMMA 4.2
1498
1499   In this section, we aim to provide a high-probability guarantee for the approximation error of the
1500   lower-level variable, namely ∥yt − yt∗ ∥. Our main technical contribution is the any-sequence argu-
1501   ment, which separates the randomness in the updates of the upper-level variable xt and the lower-
1502   level variable yt . Specifically, for any given sequence {x̃t }, we consider the following update rule
1503   for {ỹt } (which is the same as line 5 of Algorithm 1):
1504
                                             ỹt+1 = ỹt − γ∇y G(x̃t , ỹt ; ζ̃t ).                     (23)

       E     C ONVERGENCE A NALYSIS OF VR-A DAM BO (A LGORITHM 2)
2654
       In this section, we provide convergence analysis for VR-AdamBO (Algorithm 2). Before presenting
2655
       the lemmas and the main theorem, we first define (or restate) a few key concepts and useful notations.
2656
2657
       E.1    T ECHNICAL D EFINITIONS AND U SEFUL N OTATIONS
2658
2659   Filtration. Let σ(·) be the σ-algebra generated by the random variables within the argument. De-
2660   fine Finit as the filtration for updating y1 (i.e., the filtration of warm-start phase):
2661
                                          Finit = σ(π̃0 , . . . , π̃T0 −1 ),
2662                          x
2663
       For any t ≥ 2, define Ft as the filtration of the randomness used in updating xt before the t-th
       iteration:
2664
                                             Ftx = σ(S1 , ξ¯2 , . . . , ξ¯t−1 ),
2665                y
       also define Ft as the filtration of the randomness used in updating yt when t is a multiple of I:
2666
2667                                           Fty = σ(πt0 , . . . , πtN −1 ).
2668   Additionally, let Ft denote the filtration of all randomness before the t-th iteration:
2669
                                            Ft = σ(Finit ∪ Ftx ∪ (∪k<t Fky )).
2670
2671   Expectation. We use Et [·] to denote the conditional expectation E[· | Ft ].
2672
2673   Other Definitions. We define the deviation of the momentum from the conditional expectation of
2674   the hypergradient estimator as
2675                                                   ˆ            ¯
                                      ϵt := mt − Et [∇ϕ(x  t , yt ; ξt )].                       (78)
2676
2677   Also, let ht be the learning rate vector and Ht be the learning rate matrix:
2678
                                               η
                                   ht := √              and      Ht := diag(ht ).
2679                                        v̂t + λ
2680   Then the update rule for upper-level variable xt (line 18 of Algorithm 2) can be written as
2681                                      xt+1 = xt − ht ⊙ m̂t = xt − Ht m̂t .
2682
2683   Stopping Time. Given a large enough constant G as defined in Theorem E.14, denote L and ψ as
2684                                                                 CL G2
2685                                     L = L0 + L1 G       and       ψ=  ,
                                                                      2L
2686
       where constants L0 , L1 and CL are defined in (14) and (43). Now we formally define the stopping
2687
       time τ as
2688                   τ := min{t | Φ(xt ) − Φ∗ > ψ} ∧ min{t | ∥ϵt ∥ > G} ∧ (T + 1).               (79)
2689
       Based on Lemma D.1, we know if t < τ , we have Φ(xt ) − Φ∗ ≤ ψ, ∥ϵt ∥ ≤ G and ∥∇Φ(xt )∥ ≤ G.
2690
2691   Constants. We define the following constants, which will be useful for analysis.
2692
                                                                      lg,1 lf,0               512e∆1 σϕ LG2

       Theorem E.14. Suppose that Assumptions 3.2, 3.3 and 3.5 hold. Let G be a constant satisfying

                                 (                                              )

                                                   Cϕ,1     C1 ∆1 L0 C1 ∆1 L1

       F       M ORE E XPERIMENTAL D ETAILS
3536
       F.1     RNN R ESULTS ON AUC M AXIMIZATION
3537
3538   The results with RNNs for both training and testing over 25 epochs are presented in Figure 3 (a) and
3539   (b), while the corresponding running times are shown in Figure 3 (c) and (d). Our proposed Adam-
3540   type algorithms, AdamBO and VR-AdamBO, show the faster convergence rate and significantly
3541   outperform other baselines during training process.
3542
3543   F.2     H YERPARAMETER S ETTINGS FOR D EEP AUC M AXMIZATION
3544
3545   We tune the best hyperparameters for each algorithm, including upper-/lower-level step size, the
3546
       number of inner loops, momentum parameters, etc. The upper-level learning rate η and the lower-
       level learning rate γ are tuned in a wide range of [1.0×10−6 , 0.1] for all the baselines on experiments
3547
       of AUC maximization.
3548
3549   AUC maximization on transformer. The best learning rates (η, γ) are summarized as fol-
3550   lows: Stocbio: (0.005, 0.0001), TTSA: (0.0005, 0.001), SABA: (0.001, 0.005), MA-SOBA:
3551   (0.0005, 0.005), SUSTAIN: (0.005, 0.001), VRBO: (0.005, 0.0005), BO-REP: (0.0001, 0.0001),
3552
       SLIP: (0.0001, 0.001), AccBO: (0.0005, 0.0001), AdamBO: (5.0 × 10−6 , 0.005), VR-AdamBO:
       (5.0 × 10−6 , 0.005). Note that SUSTAIN decays its upper-/lower-level step size with epoch (t) by
3553
3554
       η = η/(t + 2)1/3 , ηlow = γ/(t + 2)1/3 . Other algorithms use a constant learning rate.
3555   AUC maximization on RNN. The best learning rates (η, γ) are summarized as follows:
3556   StocBio: (0.01, 0.001), TTSA: (0.005, 0.01), SABA: (0.01, 0.005), MA-SOBA: (0.01, 0.005),
3557   SUSTAIN: (0.03, 0.01), VRBO: (0.05, 0.01), BO-REP: (0.001, 0.001), SLIP: (0.001, 0.001), Ac-
3558   cBO: (0.005, 0.005), AdamBO: (1.0 × 10−5 , 0.001), VR-AdamBO: (1.0 × 10−5 , 0.001).
3559   Other hyper-parameter settings are summarized as follows. The steps for neumann series estimation
3560   in StocBiO, VRBO, AdamBO, and VR-AdamBO is set to 3, while it is uniformly sampled from
3561   {1, 2, 3} in TTSA, SUSTAIN, and AccBO. AccBO and VR-AdamBO use the Nesterov accelerated
3562   gradient descent for lower-level update, the momentum parameter α = 0.5 for AccBO and α = 0.1
3563   for VR-AdamBO, the averaging parameter ν = 0.5 for AccBO and ν = 0.1 for VR-AdamBO.
       The batch size is set to 32 for all algorithms except VRBO, which uses a larger batch size of 64

3584   F.3                    H YERPARAMETER S ETTINGS FOR H YPER - REPRESENTATION
3585
3586
       The upper-level learning rate η and the lower-level learning rate γ are tuned in a range of [1.0 ×
       10−4 , 0.1] for all the baselines. The optimal learning rate pairs are listed as follows, (0.01, 0.01) for
3587
       MAML, (0.01, 0.05) for ANIL, (0.01, 0.01) for StocBio, (0.02, 0.05) for TTSA, (0.01, 0.05) for
3588
       SABA, (0.05, 0.05) for MA-SOBA, and (0.1, 0.05) for both BO-REP and SLIP, (1.0 × 10−4 , 1.0 ×
3589   10−3 ) for AdamBO.
3590
3591   Other hyper-parameter settings are summarized as follows. The steps for neumann series estima-
3592
       tion in StocBiO, AdamBO is set to 3, while it is uniformly sampled from {1, 2, 3} in TTSA. The
       momentum parameter β = 0.1 is fixed in SLIP, MA-SOBA, BO-REP, AdamBO, and βsq = 0.001
3593
       in AdamBO. The warm start steps for the lower level variable in BO-REP, SLIP, AdamBO are set
3594
       to 3. The number of inner loops for StocBio is set to 3. BO-REP uses the periodic update for the
3595   low-level variable, and sets the iterations N = 3 and the update interval I = 2. The hyperparameter
3596   λ in the Adam update is fixed as 1.0 × 10−8 in AdamBO and VR-AdamBO.

3601   Assumption G.1. Consider the following smoothness assumptions:
3602
3603   (A) The objective function is L-smooth.
3604   (B) The objective function is (L0 , L1 )-smooth (Zhang et al., 2020a, Definition 1.1, Remark 2.3).
3605
3606   (C) The objective function is (ρ, L0 , Lρ )-smooth with 0 ≤ ρ < 2 (Li et al., 2023a, Definition 3.2).
3607
3608   The above assumptions satisfy: Assumption G.1(A) =⇒ Assumption G.1(B) =⇒ Assumption G.1(C).
3609   In other words, Assumption G.1(A) is the strongest, and Assumption G.1(C) is the weakest.
3610   Assumption G.2. The (stochastic) gradient norm of the objective function is (almost surely)
3611   bounded.
3612   Assumption G.3. Suppose the following stochastic estimators are unbiased and satisfy:

3646   H         A DDITIONAL E XPERIMENTS
3647
3648   H.1         M ETA -L EARNING ON BERT
3649
       We have conducted meta-learning experiments on a larger language model, specifically an 8-layer
3650
       BERT (Devlin et al., 2018) model. The experiments are performed on a widely-used question clas-
3651   sification dataset TREC (Li & Roth, 2002), which contains 6 coarse-grained categories. To evaluate
3652   our approach on meta-learning, we construct K = 500 meta tasks, where the training data Ditr and
3653   validation data Dival for the i-th task are randomly sampled from two disjoint categories, with 5
3654   examples per category. A BERT model, with 8 self-attention layers and a fully-connected layer, is
3655   used in our experiment. The self-attention layers serve as representation layers (with their param-
3656   eters treated as upper-level variables) and the fully-connected layer (with its parameters treated as
3657   lower-level variables) serves as an adapter, where each self-attention layer consists of 8 self-attention
3658   heads with the hidden size being 768. The fully-connected layer acts as a classifier, with the input
3659   dimension of 768 and the output dimension of 6 (corresponding to the 6 categories). Our bilevel
3660
       optimization algorithm trains the representation layers and the adapter on the meta tasks (Dtr and
       Dval ) from scratch, and then evaluate it on the test set Dte . During the evaluation phase, we fix
3661
       the parameters of representation layers and just fine-tune the adapters. We train the models for 20
3662
       epochs and compare it with other bilevel optimization baseline algorithms. The training and testing
3663   comparison results are presented in Figure 4. As shown, the proposed algorithm AdamBO achieves
3664   fast convergence to the best training and test results among all baselines.
3665
3666
       The best upper-level learning rate η and lower-level learning rate γ for all baselines are tuned. The
       detailed settings are summarized as following:
3667
3668        2
            (Guo et al., 2021b, Assumption 2) can be implied by Assumption G.2, although it is weaker.
3669        3
            Adam can converge with an additional strong growth condition (Zhang et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2022).
          4
3670        Under Assumption 3.2, the objective function Φ is (L0 , L1 )-smooth, see Lemma B.10 for details.
          5
3671        The single-loop version (Option I) of AccBO (Gong et al., 2024b) only works for one-dimensional
       quadratic lower-level function.

        H.2                                S ENSITIVITY TO THE C HOICE OF λ
3708
3709   In addition, we have conducted additional experiments in Figure 5 to show the empirical perfor-
3710   mance of our algorithm is not very sensitive to the choice of λ. Although the default choice of λ is
3711   10−8 (Kingma & Ba, 2014), increasing it up to 10−4 only causes minor differences in AUC maxi-
3712   mization, and increasing it up to 10−3 leads to minor changes in hyper-representation performance
3713   with BERT (Devlin et al., 2018).
3714
3715
        I                     P ROOF S KETCH FOR VR-A DAM BO (T HEOREM 5.1)
3716
3717
       For VR-AdamBO, we provide a more detailed proof sketch here due to space constraints in the main
3718   text. In particular, we present two main challenges and outline the proof roadmap to address them.
3719
3720   Challenge 1: VR-AdamBO vs. VRAdam (Li et al., 2023a). The analysis of VRAdam in the
3721   single-level generalized smooth optimization setting (Li et al., 2023a) is not directly applicable to
3722   bilevel problems. This is because the hypergradient estimator in bilevel optimization may have a
3723   non-negligible bias due to inaccurate estimation of the lower-level variable, whereas the single-level
3724   analysis in (Li et al., 2023a) does not need to account for this issue. To control the lower-level
3725   estimation error, we leverage the lower-level acceleration technique (Gong et al., 2024b) with peri-
       odic updates and averaging. While we largely adopt the framework of VRAdam for the upper-level


                                                                                                                                                  69
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3726
       analysis, the main distinction lies in our incorporation of the hypergradient bias—arising from inac-
3727   curate estimates of the optimal lower-level variable at each iteration—into the upper-level analysis.
3728   This is detailed in Lemmas E.5 to E.8 of our paper, which correspond to Lemmas D.4, D.6, D.7, and
3729   D.8 in (Li et al., 2023a), respectively.
3730
3731   Challenge 2: VR-AdamBO vs. AccBO (Gong et al., 2024b). Although both VR-AdamBO and
3732   AccBO Gong et al. (2024b) use the same lower-level update (periodic SNAG with averaging) and
3733   adopt the same variance reduction technique STORM (Cutkosky & Orabona, 2019) for the first-
3734   order momentum update, the key difference between these two algorithms lies in the upper-level
3735   update: AccBO uses normalized SGD with momentum, while VR-AdamBO employs VRAdam.
3736   This distinction leads to significantly different theoretical analyses for VR-AdamBO and AccBO. In
3737
       particular, for VR-AdamBO, we introduce a novel stopping time approach in the context of bilevel
       optimization (see equation (7) in Section 5.3), building on the VR-Adam analysis Li et al. (2023a).
3738
       Base on the definition of stopping time τ , we develop a new induction argument (i.e., Lemmas E.10
3739
       to E.12) to show that under t < τ and some good event Ey (see Lemma E.10 for definition), both
3740   ∥ŷt − yt∗ ∥ and ∥mt ∥ are bounded. We then show the averaged lower-level error is small (see Lemma
3741   E.4) under the parameter choices in Theorem 5.1, which shares an similar spirit as Lemma 4.6 for
3742   AdamBO. Combining the aforementioned lemmas with the upper-level analysis mentioned above in
3743   Challenge 1 (i.e., Lemmas E.5 to E.8), we can obtain the improved O(ϵ   e −3 ) complexity result.
